    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="21b2d8bc9f74476bb476bba95d3a111e">

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: '235230aa-4429-4310-bf9b-61e192d323a2'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

   <div class="abstract-group ">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en">
         <h2 id="d60345417" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>High-entropy layered oxide materials containing various metals that exhibit smooth voltage curves and excellent electrochemical performances have attracted attention in the development of positive electrode materials for sodium-ion batteries. However, a smooth voltage curve can be obtained by suppression of the Na<sup>+</sup>-vacancy ordering, and therefore, transition metal slabs do not need to be more multi-element than necessary. Here, the Na<sup>+</sup>-vacancy ordering is found to be disturbed by dual substitution of Ti<sup>IV</sup> for Mn<sup>IV</sup> and Zn<sup>II</sup> for Ni<sup>II</sup> in P2-Na<sub>2/3</sub>[Ni<sub>1/3</sub>Mn<sub>2/3</sub>]O<sub>2</sub>. Dual-substituted Na<sub>2/3</sub>[Ni<sub>1/4</sub>Mn<sub>1/2</sub>Ti<sub>1/6</sub>Zn<sub>1/12</sub>]O<sub>2</sub> demonstrates almost non-step voltage curves with a reversible capacity of 114&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> and less structural changes with a high crystalline structure maintained during charging and discharging. Synchrotron X-ray, neutron, and electron diffraction measurements reveal that dual-substitution with Ti<sup>IV</sup> and Zn<sup>II</sup> uniquely promotes in-plane Ni<sup>II</sup>–Mn<sup>IV</sup> ordering, which is quite different from the disordered mixing in conventional multiple metal substitution.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adma202300714-sec-0010">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adma202300714-sec-0010-title">1 Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>Na-ion batteries have recently attracted much attention for battery researchers<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202300714-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202300714-bib-0001_R_d60345409e396" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Adv. Energy Mater.">1</a>-<a href="#adma202300714-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202300714-bib-0003_R_d60345409e396" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - Nat. Rev. Mater.">3</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> since acceptable cycle stability was achieved by hard carbon||O3 type Na[Ni<sub>1/2</sub>Mn<sub>1/2</sub>]O<sub>2</sub> full cell in 2009.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202300714-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202300714-bib-0004_R_d60345409e408" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">4</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Owing to the rapid and significant developments of carbonous electrode materials, the negative electrode have recently achieved an extremely large reversible capacity of ≈480&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202300714-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202300714-bib-0005_R_d60345409e415" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">5</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Therefore, to realize environmental-friendly and low-cost Na-ion batteries, development of higher energy positive electrode materials are of significant importance to increase the energy density of Na-ion cells and decrease the usage of expensive separators and non-aqueous electrolytes.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202300714-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202300714-bib-0003_R_d60345409e421" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - Nat. Rev. Mater.">3</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>Among sodium layered oxides, P2 type Na<sub>2/3</sub>[Ni<sub>1/3</sub>Mn<sub>2/3</sub>]O<sub>2</sub> has a unique <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/bf3b4901-5fa9-4b79-bae1-9a9b3b2afd7e/adma202300714-math-0001.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:09359648:media:adma202300714:adma202300714-math-0001" display="inline" location="graphic/adma202300714-math-0001.png">
               <semantics>
                  <mrow>
                     <msqrt>
                        <mn>3</mn>
                     </msqrt>
                     <mspace width="0.16em"></mspace>
                     <mo>×</mo>
                     <mspace width="0.16em"></mspace>
                     <msqrt>
                        <mn>3</mn>
                     </msqrt>
                  </mrow>
                  <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">$\sqrt {\rm{3}} \,{\rm{ \times }}\,\sqrt {\rm{3}} $</annotation>
               </semantics></math> superlattice structure with an in-plane Ni<sup>II</sup>–Mn<sup>IV</sup> honeycomb ordering and high water-stability<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202300714-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202300714-bib-0006_R_d60345409e454" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - Chem. Mater.">6</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> enabling facile handling in the battery assembly process. Electrochemical Na intercalation performance is also unique, almost all Na<sup>+</sup> ions can be extracted by charging to 4.5&nbsp;V versus Na and reversibly intercalated into the structure by discharging. P2 type Na<sub>2/3</sub>[Ni<sub>1/3</sub>Mn<sub>2/3</sub>]O<sub>2</sub> delivers a high reversible capacity of ≈150&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> with a high discharge-voltage during a few cycles. However, the large reversible capacity rapidly deteriorates charge–discharge cycling.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202300714-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202300714-bib-0007_R_d60345409e472" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - J. Electrochem. Soc.">7</a>, <a href="#adma202300714-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202300714-bib-0008_R_d60345409e472" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Phys. Chem. Chem. Phys.">8</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The severe capacity decay is thought to be due to severe volume change in the phase transition from P2 Na<i><sub>x</sub></i>[Ni<sub>1/3</sub>Mn<sub>2/3</sub>]O<sub>2</sub> to O2 type [Ni<sub>1/3</sub>Mn<sub>2/3</sub>]O<sub>2</sub> on charging to above 4.1&nbsp;V,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202300714-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202300714-bib-0008_R_d60345409e494" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Phys. Chem. Chem. Phys.">8</a>, <a href="#adma202300714-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202300714-bib-0009_R_d60345409e494" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Chem. Commun.">9</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> and many researchers have devoted much effort on the improvement mainly using metal substitution.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202300714-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202300714-bib-0009_R_d60345409e499" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Chem. Commun.">9</a>-<a href="#adma202300714-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202300714-bib-0014_R_d60345409e499" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Energy Storage Mater.">14</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>For the metal substitution, our group has emphasized the general trend that suitable valence state of metals and metal ratio exist in layered composite oxides like M<sup>II</sup> and M<sup>IV</sup> in Na<sub>2/3</sub>[M<sup>II</sup><sub>1/3</sub>M<sup>IV</sup><sub>2/3</sub>]O<sub>2</sub> (M<sup>II</sup> = Mg, Ni, Zn, Cu, M<sup>IV</sup> = Mn, Ti, Sn).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202300714-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202300714-bib-0015_R_d60345409e525" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - Adv. Energy Mater.">15</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> As we earlier reported in 2014, partial Ti<sup>IV</sup> substitution for Mn<sup>IV</sup> in Na<sub>2/3</sub>[Ni<sub>1/3</sub>Mn<sub>2/3</sub>]O<sub>2</sub> successfully suppresses the drastic volume change during charging of Na<i><sub>x</sub></i>[Ni<sub>1/3</sub>Mn<sub>2/3</sub>]O<sub>2</sub> and improves cycle stability. Furthermore, the Ti substitution changes shape of voltage curves from stepwise into sloping and also increases average operating voltage.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202300714-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202300714-bib-0009_R_d60345409e553" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Chem. Commun.">9</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The decrease in the number of voltage steps is known to originate from the suppression of Na<sup>+</sup>-vacancy ordering and the decrease in the number of phase transitions during charge–discharge. This is caused by doped redox-inactive metals such as Ti<sup>IV</sup>, whose Fermi level in the oxide is different from that of redox-active metals.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202300714-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202300714-bib-0016_R_d60345409e563" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Nat. Commun.">16</a>, <a href="#adma202300714-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202300714-bib-0017_R_d60345409e563" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - Sci. Adv.">17</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The Na<sup>+</sup>-vacancy ordering is known to be governed by the complex interaction between Na<sup>+</sup> and Na<sup>+</sup> repulsion, Na<sup>+</sup> and TM<sup>+</sup> interaction, and TM charge ordering (TM = transition metals),<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202300714-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202300714-bib-0018_R_d60345409e579" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - J. Phys. Chem. C">18</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> and evidenced by X-ray diffraction (XRD) and neutron diffraction (ND) measurements.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202300714-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202300714-bib-0019_R_d60345409e585" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - Phys. Rev. B: Condens. Matter Mater. Phys.">19</a>-<a href="#adma202300714-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202300714-bib-0023_R_d60345409e585" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - J. Mater. Chem. A">23</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Indeed, the multi-elementalization and disordering of metals in the slabs leads to a sloping voltage curve and excellent electrochemical properties. High-entropy materials containing various metals<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202300714-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202300714-bib-0024_R_d60345409e590" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">24</a>-<a href="#adma202300714-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202300714-bib-0026_R_d60345409e590" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - Adv. Energy Mater.">26</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> such as NaNi<sub>0.12</sub>Cu<sub>0.12</sub>Mg<sub>0.12</sub>Fe<sub>0.15</sub>Co<sub>0.15</sub>Mn<sub>0.1</sub>Ti<sub>0.1</sub>Sn<sub>0.1</sub>Sb<sub>0.04</sub>O<sub>2</sub><sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202300714-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202300714-bib-0024_R_d60345409e616" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">24</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> have attracted attention in the recent development of positive electrode materials for Na-ion batteries. Although multi-elementalization of the metals should not be necessary in terms of suppressing Na<sup>+</sup>-vacancy ordering, the relationship between the Na<sup>+</sup>-vacancy ordering and the atomic arrangement of 3d transiton metals with the dopted multi-element is not fully understood because 3d transition metals cannot be distinguished by XRD.</p>
         
         <p>To fully understand the effect of multi-elements substitution, in this study, Mn<sup>IV</sup> and Ni<sup>II</sup> in P2 type Na<sub>2/3</sub>[Ni<sub>1/3</sub>Mn<sub>2/3</sub>]O<sub>2</sub> were co-substituted with Ti<sup>IV</sup> and Me<sup>II</sup> (Me<sup>II</sup> = Mg, Zn, Cu), respectively, and the correlation between their crystal structures (including Ni–Mn and Na<sup>+</sup>-vacancy ordering) and electrochemical properties were investigated. Novel Ti<sup>IV</sup> and Zn<sup>II</sup> dual-substituted Na<sub>2/3</sub>[Ni<sub>1/3</sub>Mn<sub>2/3</sub>]O<sub>2</sub> has demonstrated non-stepwise charge–discharge curves and delivering a reversible capacity of 114&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> and high voltage of 3.60&nbsp;V with excellent cycle stability. XRD, ND, and electron diffraction (ED) measurements were used to determine the effect of Ti<sup>IV</sup> and Zn<sup>II</sup> dual-substitution on Ni<sup>II</sup>–Mn<sup>IV</sup> ordering and Na<sup>+</sup>-vacancy ordering. Furthermore, operando XRD and transmission electron microscopy (TEM) observation were carried out to elucidate highly stable crystalline structures of Ti<sup>IV</sup> and Zn<sup>II</sup> dual-substituted Na<sub>2/3</sub>[Ni<sub>1/3</sub>Mn<sub>2/3</sub>]O<sub>2</sub>.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adma202300714-sec-0020">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adma202300714-sec-0020-title">2 Results and Discussion</h2>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adma202300714-sec-0030">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="adma202300714-sec-0030-title">2.1 Na<sub>2/3</sub>[Ni<sub>1/4</sub>Mn<sub>1/2</sub>Me<sub>1/12</sub>Ti<sub>1/6</sub>]O<sub>2</sub> (Me = Mg, Cu, Zn)</h3>
            
            <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adma202300714-sec-0040">
               
               <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section3" id="adma202300714-sec-0040-title">2.1.1 Structural Characterization</h4>
               
               <p>Na<sub>2/3</sub>[Ni<sub>1/3−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>Mn<sub>1/2</sub>Me<i><sub>x</sub></i>Ti<sub>1/6</sub>]O<sub>2</sub> (Me = Mg, Cu, and Zn, <i>x</i> = 0 and 1/12) were prepared by a solid-state reaction of Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub> and metal oxides (see the details in the experimental section). The synthesized samples of Na<sub>2/3</sub>[Ni<sub>1/3</sub>Mn<sub>2/3</sub>]O<sub>2</sub>, Na<sub>2/3</sub>[Ni<sub>1/3</sub>Mn<sub>1/2</sub>Ti<sub>1/6</sub>]O<sub>2</sub>, Na<sub>2/3</sub>[Ni<sub>1/4</sub>Mn<sub>1/2</sub>Ti<sub>1/6</sub>Cu<sub>1/12</sub>]O<sub>2</sub>, Na<sub>2/3</sub>[Ni<sub>1/4</sub>Mn<sub>1/2</sub>Ti<sub>1/6</sub>Mg<sub>1/12</sub>]O<sub>2</sub>, and Na<sub>2/3</sub>[Ni<sub>1/4</sub>Mn<sub>1/2</sub>Ti<sub>1/6</sub>Zn<sub>1/12</sub>]O<sub>2</sub> are, hereinafter, referred to as “Non-sub”, “Ti-sub”, “Ti-Cu-sub”, “Ti-Mg-sub”, and “Ti-Zn-sub”, respectively. Crystal structures of the synthesized Ti-substituted samples were identified by synchrotron XRD measurements. <b>Figure</b> <a href="#adma202300714-fig-0001">1a</a> displays synchrotron powder XRD patterns of as-prepared Ti-sub, Ti-Mg-sub, Ti-Cu-sub, and Ti-Zn-sub samples. Observed Bragg diffraction peaks can be mainly indexed as an P2 type layered phase with a space group of <i>P</i>6<sub>3</sub>/<i>mmc</i>, which is isostructural to RbScO<sub>2</sub>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202300714-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202300714-bib-0027_R_d60345409e816" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - Rev. Chim. Miner.">27</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Only in the case of Ti-Zn-sub sample, an impurity phase is detected and diffraction peaks at 34.4° and 36.3° in diffraction angles of 2<i>θ</i> are assigned to ZnO (ICSD#26170).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202300714-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202300714-bib-0028_R_d60345409e823" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - Acta Crystallogr., Sect. B: Struct. Crystallogr. Cryst. Chem.">28</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Even when the targeted Zn content <i>x</i> was reduced from 1/12 to 1/24 in Na<sub>2/3</sub>[Ni<sub>1/3−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>Mn<sub>1/2</sub>Zn<i><sub>x</sub></i>Ti<sub>1/6</sub>]O<sub>2</sub>, the diffraction peaks of ZnO are still detected as an impurity phase (see Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S</a>1 in the Supporting Information), indicating that the amount of Zn in the layered oxide (i.e., solid solution limit of Zn) is very small.</p>
               
               <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
                  <figure class="figure" id="adma202300714-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/369dd822-ed30-4c48-adb1-0c5e63dab7d5/adma202300714-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                           <source srcset="/cms/asset/369dd822-ed30-4c48-adb1-0c5e63dab7d5/adma202300714-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/c763bc27-725e-4c17-91f8-e702d269a64c/adma202300714-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/369dd822-ed30-4c48-adb1-0c5e63dab7d5/adma202300714-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">

                     </figcaption>
                  </figure>
               </section>
               <p>The superlattice peaks indexed as 1/6 2/3 <i>l</i> reflections are quite weak but are observed in the diffraction angle region of 2<i>θ</i> = 8–10° (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202300714-fig-0001">1b</a>). These superlattice peaks are mainly attributed to Na<sup>+</sup>-vacancy ordering as reported for P2-Na<sub>2/3</sub>CoO<sub>2</sub> and Non-sub,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202300714-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202300714-bib-0008_R_d60345409e1011" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Phys. Chem. Chem. Phys.">8</a>, <a href="#adma202300714-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202300714-bib-0017_R_d60345409e1011" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - Sci. Adv.">17</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> while the superlattice peaks attributed to the in-plane TM ordering in the TMO<sub>2</sub> slab in 2<i>θ</i> = 6–8° are indexed as 1/3 1/3 <i>l</i> reflections. The latter superlattice peaks are visible in the XRD patterns only for light elemental substitution such as P2-Na<sub>5/6</sub>[Li<sub>1/4</sub>Mn<sub>3/4</sub>]O<sub>2</sub><sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202300714-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202300714-bib-0029_R_d60345409e1030" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - Adv. Energy Mater.">29</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> and P2-Na<i><sub>x</sub></i>[Mg,Mn]O<sub>2</sub>,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202300714-bib-0030" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202300714-bib-0030_R_d60345409e1041" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 30 - Chem. Mater.">30</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> but are visible in the neutron diffraction patterns for almost any elemental substitution. The superlattice peaks of the Ni–Mn ordered (<span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/21324f8a-af44-4e9f-ab30-986b7e7b284f/adma202300714-math-0002.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:09359648:media:adma202300714:adma202300714-math-0002" display="inline" location="graphic/adma202300714-math-0002.png">

Phys.">20</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, the superlattice peaks in the XRD pattern of P2-Na<sub>2/3</sub>CoO<sub>2</sub> are different from Non-sub and substituted samples (see Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2b</a>, Supporting Information). One plausible reason for this is the difference in the stacking structure of Na<sup>+</sup>-vacancy layers, which correlates with the stacking sequence of TMO<sub>2</sub> slabs.</p>

Mater.">30</a>, <a href="#adma202300714-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202300714-bib-0031_R_d60345409e1257" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 31 - Chem. Mater.">31</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The detailed crystal structures of Non-sub and Ti-sub are discussed in detail in a later section based on the results of ND measurements. Here, a comparison based on XRD patterns suggests that Ti-Mg-sub and Ti-Zn-sub show similar peak profile of 1/6 2/3 <i>l</i> peaks, indicating similar Na<sup>+</sup>-vacancy ordering and stacking. In contrast, Ti-Cu-sub and Ti-sub represent broader 1/6 2/3 <i>l</i> peaks, suggesting that Na<sup>+</sup>-vacancy ordering is present with abundant stacking faults. Despite the differences in the peak profile of the superlattice peaks, all four Ti–Me-substituted samples exhibit similar morphology, hexagonal platelet particles a few microns in diameter as shown in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#adma202300714-fig-0002">2</a>.</p>
               
               <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
                  <figure class="figure" id="adma202300714-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/9d3b423a-57e9-46e3-8c7a-dbdfae662ed4/adma202300714-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                           <source srcset="/cms/asset/9d3b423a-57e9-46e3-8c7a-dbdfae662ed4/adma202300714-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/7641ce79-f1f3-48c5-b673-73b2672ab07e/adma202300714-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/9d3b423a-57e9-46e3-8c7a-dbdfae662ed4/adma202300714-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                        <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma202300714-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.202300714" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>

                     </figcaption>

            <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adma202300714-sec-0050">
               
               <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section3" id="adma202300714-sec-0050-title">2.1.2 Electrochemical Properties</h4>
               
               <p>Electrochemical properties of the Na<sub>2/3</sub>[Ni<sub>1/3−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>Mn<sub>1/2</sub>Me<i><sub>x</sub></i>Ti<sub>1/6</sub>]O<sub>2</sub> electrodes were examined in non-aqueous Na cells filled with 1.0&nbsp;mol&nbsp;dm<sup>−3</sup> NaPF<sub>6</sub>/PC electrolyte. <b>Figure</b> <a href="#adma202300714-fig-0003">3a–d</a> displays galvanostatic charge/discharge curves in the wide voltage range of 2.5-4.5&nbsp;V at a current density of 13&nbsp;mA&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> for 40 cycles. Compared to Non-sub (160&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202300714-fig-0003">3e</a>), Ti-sub, Ti-Mg-sub, Ti-Cu-sub, and Ti-Zn-sub electrodes deliver smaller discharge capacities of 115–120&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> at the initial cycle. Among them, the Ti-sub electrode exhibits pronounced stepwise voltage curves as observed in the case of Non-sub.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202300714-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202300714-bib-0009_R_d60345409e1340" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Chem. Commun.">9</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Such voltage steps are known to be caused by the ordering of sodium and vacancy in the interslab space and the simultaneous charge ordering (or dimerization or trimerization) of transition metal ions at the specific sodium content, such as <i>x</i> = 2/3, 1/2, and 1/3 in Na<i><sub>x</sub></i>TMO<sub>2</sub>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202300714-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202300714-bib-0008_R_d60345409e1353" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Phys. Chem. Chem. Phys.">8</a>, <a href="#adma202300714-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202300714-bib-0032_R_d60345409e1353" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 32 - Nat. Mater.">32</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In contrast, the dual-substituted samples with Ti and divalent metals represent smooth voltage curves. Distinct voltage-jump and -drop are seen only at the beginning and the end of charge and discharge. Although the initial charge–discharge profiles of the dual-substituted samples are similar, Ti-Cu-sub and Ti-Zn-sub electrodes demonstrate superior reversibility and excellent capacity retention for 40 cycles (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202300714-fig-0003">3e</a>). Despite the limited solubility of Zn in a solid-solution and its segregation as a ZnO particle, Zn-substitution is remarkably effective on smooth charge–discharge voltage profiles and improvent of cycle stability (see Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a>, Supporting Information). Excellent capacity retention is achieved in the cases of Zn-rich Ti-Zn-sub samples (<i>x</i> = 1/20 and 1/12 in Na<sub>2/3</sub>[Ni<sub>1/3−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>Mn<sub>1/2</sub>Zn<i><sub>x</sub></i>Ti<sub>1/6</sub>]O<sub>2</sub>). When rate performance of Ti–Me-substituted samples is further compared (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202300714-fig-0003">3f</a>), Ti-sub and Ti-Mg-sub electrodes exhibit better rate-capability than Non-sub as previously reported.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202300714-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202300714-bib-0014_R_d60345409e1386" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Energy Storage Mater.">14</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Ti-Cu-sub and Ti-Zn-sub electrodes demonstrate further superior rate-performance and deliver more than 90% of the maximum reversible capacity at 2C-rate, which is much higher than Non-sub (33% retention). Furthermore, the Ti-Zn-sub electrode demonstrates high cycle-stability in the voltage ranges of 1.0–4.5&nbsp;V and 2.5–4.8&nbsp;V as well as 2.5–4.5&nbsp;V (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4</a>, Supporting Information). The stability in the wider voltage ranges is much better than that of Non-sub reported previously.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202300714-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202300714-bib-0008_R_d60345409e1395" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Phys. Chem. Chem. Phys.">8</a>, <a href="#adma202300714-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202300714-bib-0009_R_d60345409e1395" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Chem. Commun.">9</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
               
               <section class="article-section__inline-figure">

         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adma202300714-sec-0060">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="adma202300714-sec-0060-title">2.2 Na<sub>2/3</sub>[Ni<sub>1/3−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>Mn<sub>2/3−</sub><i><sub>y</sub></i>Zn<i><sub>x</sub></i>Ti<i><sub>y</sub></i>]O<sub>2</sub></h3>
            
            <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adma202300714-sec-0070">
               
               <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section3" id="adma202300714-sec-0070-title">2.2.1 Na<sup>+</sup>-Vacancy Ordering and Ni–Mn Ordering</h4>
               
               <p>Focusing on Ti-Zn-sub, which demonstrated the best electrochemical performance among the Ti–Me-substituted samples, we will now discuss the effect of Ti-substitution and Ti–Zn dual-substitution. First, we compared ND patterns of Na<sub>2/3</sub>[Ni<sub>1/3</sub>Mn<sub>2/3−</sub><i><sub>y</sub></i>Ti<i><sub>y</sub></i>]O<sub>2</sub> (<i>y</i> = 0, 1/6, 1/3, and 2/3) and Ti-Zn-sub in order to understand the Ni–Mn and Na<sup>+</sup>-vacancy ordering as shown in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#adma202300714-fig-0004">4a</a>. Similar to the previous report,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202300714-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202300714-bib-0018_R_d60345409e1477" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - J. Phys. Chem. C">18</a>, <a href="#adma202300714-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202300714-bib-0031_R_d60345409e1477" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 31 - Chem. Mater.">31</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> the ND pattern of Non-sub represents 1/3 1/3 <i>l</i> (<i>l</i> = 0, 1, 2, and 3) reflections in the <i>d</i>-spacing range of 2.7–4.5&nbsp;Å, attributed to <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/01730885-ab8b-4d9b-9e34-a65b799b04c9/adma202300714-math-0009.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:09359648:media:adma202300714:adma202300714-math-0009" display="inline" location="graphic/adma202300714-math-0009.png">
                     <semantics>
                        <mrow>
                           <msqrt>
                              <mn>3</mn>
                           </msqrt>
                           <mo>×</mo>
                           <msqrt>
                              <mn>3</mn>
                           </msqrt>
                        </mrow>
                        <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">$\sqrt {\rm{3}} {\rm{ \times }}\sqrt {\rm{3}} $</annotation>
                     </semantics></math> superlattice with an in-plane honeycomb ordering of Ni–Mn in a slab and a stacking sequence of A<sub>2</sub>A<sub>3</sub>A<sub>2</sub>A<sub>3</sub>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202300714-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202300714-bib-0031_R_d60345409e1509" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 31 - Chem. Mater.">31</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In addition to the 1/3 1/3 <i>l</i> reflections, 1/6 2/3 <i>l</i> (<i>l</i> = 0 and 1) reflections are also observed. The former 1/3 1/3 <i>l</i> superlattice peaks are quite weak and almost negligible in the synchrotron XRD pattern (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202300714-fig-0004">4b</a>), which is due to similar X-ray scattering factors but different neutron coherent scattering lengths of Ni and Mn (10.3(1) and −3.73(2) fm, respectively<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202300714-bib-0033" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202300714-bib-0033_R_d60345409e1526" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 33 - J. Neutron Res.">33</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup>). On the other hand, the latter superlattice peaks of 1/6 2/3 <i>l</i> reflections, observed in both XRD and ND patterns, are attributed to the in-plane Na<sup>+</sup>-vacancy ordering with a (<span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/435803f9-adf8-4964-bd99-f64aea61b5f4/adma202300714-math-0010.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:09359648:media:adma202300714:adma202300714-math-0010" display="inline" location="graphic/adma202300714-math-0010.png">

                           </msqrt>

                              <mo>×</mo>
                              <mn>2</mn>
                           </mrow>
                           <msqrt>
                              <mn>3</mn>
                           </msqrt>
                        </mrow>
                        <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">${\rm{2}}\sqrt {\rm{3}} {\rm{ \times 2}}\sqrt {\rm{3}} $</annotation>
                     </semantics></math>)R30° superlattice.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202300714-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202300714-bib-0008_R_d60345409e1550" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Phys. Chem. Chem. Phys.">8</a>, <a href="#adma202300714-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202300714-bib-0018_R_d60345409e1550" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - J. Phys. Chem. C">18</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Though the calculated structural model has been reported for Non-sub having both Na<sup>+</sup>-vacancy and Ni–Mn ordering,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202300714-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202300714-bib-0008_R_d60345409e1558" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Phys. Chem. Chem. Phys.">8</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> no structural model has been proved experimentally and a stacking sequence of Na<sup>+</sup>-vacancy layers is not reported yet. Therefore, we considered both Na<sup>+</sup>-vacancy and Ni–Mn orderings on the basis of calculated model which was predicted by DFT calculations and illustrated<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202300714-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202300714-bib-0008_R_d60345409e1567" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Phys. Chem. Chem. Phys.">8</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> as shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202300714-fig-0001">1c</a>. The crystal structure of Non-sub was modeled with a superstructure unit cell <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/7c3aaccf-f9dd-4f34-9e42-0954b2fb94dd/adma202300714-math-0011.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:09359648:media:adma202300714:adma202300714-math-0011" display="inline" location="graphic/adma202300714-math-0011.png">
                     <semantics>
                        <mrow>
                           <mn>2</mn>
                           <msqrt>
                              <mn>3</mn>
                           </msqrt>
                           <mi>a</mi>
                           <mrow>
                              <mspace width="0.33em"></mspace>
                              <mo>×</mo>
                              <mspace width="0.33em"></mspace>
                              <mn>2</mn>
                           </mrow>
                           <msqrt>
                              <mn>3</mn>
                           </msqrt>
                           <mi>b</mi>
                           <mrow>
                              <mspace width="0.33em"></mspace>
                              <mo>×</mo>
                              <mspace width="0.33em"></mspace>
                           </mrow>
                           <mi>c</mi>
                        </mrow>
                        <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">${\rm{2}}\sqrt {\rm{3}} a{\rm{\ \times \ 2}}\sqrt {\rm{3}} b{\rm{\ \times \ }}c$</annotation>
                     </semantics></math> and refined for the first time by the Rietveld method for the ND pattern of Non-sub in the large <i>d</i>-spacing value region (<i>d</i> ≥ 4) obtained by a low-angle bank detector.</p>
               
               <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
                  <figure class="figure" id="adma202300714-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/a1f697f8-8686-46db-bf67-829456a4dbfe/adma202300714-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>

                        <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 4<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma202300714-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.202300714" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                        </div>
                        <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) ND and b) XRD patterns of Na<sub>2/3</sub>[Ni<sub>1/3</sub>Mn<sub>2/3</sub>]O<sub>2</sub> (Non-sub), Na<sub>2/3</sub>[Ni<sub>1/3</sub>Mn<sub>1/3</sub>Ti<sub>1/6</sub>]O<sub>2</sub> (Ti-sub), Na<sub>2/3</sub>[Ni<sub>1/3</sub>Mn<sub>1/3</sub>Ti<sub>1/3</sub>]O<sub>2</sub>, and Na<sub>2/3</sub>[Ni<sub>1/4</sub>Mn<sub>1/2</sub>Ti<sub>1/6</sub>Zn<sub>1/12</sub>]O<sub>2</sub> (Ti-Zn-sub) samples. c) Schematic illustrations of a refined hexagonal superlattice structure of Non-sub. d–f) Selected-area electron diffraction (SAED) patterns of Non-sub (d), Ti-sub (e), and Ti-Zn-sub (f) samples along the [001]<sub>hex.</sub> zone axis. g) Schematic illustrations of a refined hexagonal superlattice structure of Ti-Zn-sub. To compare the structures with that of P2-Na<sub>2/3</sub>CoO<sub>2</sub> in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a> (Supporting Information), the atomic positions in (c) and (g) were transformed and differ from the standardized structural parameters in Tables <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a> (Supporting Information), respectively.</div>
                     </figcaption>
                  </figure>
               </section>
               <p>The refinement results of the ND patterns (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5</a>, Supporting Information) and structural parameters (Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a>, Supporting Information) represent good agreement between the observed and calculated patterns. The refined structure with a space group of <i>P</i>6<sub>3</sub> has an in-plane Na<sup>+</sup>-vacancy ordering as well as an in-plane Ni–Mn ordering in the slab with the slab stacking of A<sub>2</sub>A<sub>3</sub>A<sub>2</sub>A<sub>3</sub> as shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202300714-fig-0004">4c</a>. The in-plane Na<sup>+</sup>-vacancy ordering is identical to that of the theoretically predicted model<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202300714-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202300714-bib-0008_R_d60345409e1712" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Phys. Chem. Chem. Phys.">8</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> and that of P2-Na<sub>2/3</sub>CoO<sub>2</sub>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202300714-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202300714-bib-0020_R_d60345409e1721" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - Phys. Rev. B: Condens. Matter Mater. Phys.">20</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Following the stacking sequence of Na<sup>+</sup>-vacancy layers in P2-Na<sub>2/3</sub>CoO<sub>2</sub> (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a>, Supporting Information), the overall stacking sequence in Non-sub is A<sub>3</sub><i>α</i><sub>1</sub>A<sub>2</sub><i>α</i><sub>3’</sub>A<sub>3</sub><i>α</i><sub>1</sub>A<sub>2</sub><i>α</i><sub>3’</sub>. Although the good refinement results are obtained for the ND pattern detected by a low-angle bank detector, the ND pattern detected by a high-resolution bank detector and the synchrotron XRD pattern show split <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/23f4a753-7b90-45c5-bf8f-d64530aed4a7/adma202300714-math-0012.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:09359648:media:adma202300714:adma202300714-math-0012" display="inline" location="graphic/adma202300714-math-0012.png">
                     <semantics>
                        <mrow>
                           <mn>220</mn>
                        </mrow>
                        <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">$\hskip.001pt 220$</annotation>
                     </semantics></math>, <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/1da2f0dd-3f67-4418-a024-0150c965eb87/adma202300714-math-0013.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:09359648:media:adma202300714:adma202300714-math-0013" display="inline" location="graphic/adma202300714-math-0013.png">
                     <semantics>
                        <mrow>
                           <mn>22</mn>
                           <mover>
                              <mn>1</mn>
                              <mo>¯</mo>
                           </mover>
                        </mrow>
                        <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">$22\overline{1}$</annotation>
                     </semantics></math>, and <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/05d04639-89d8-4ee0-814c-f0c671c1ce28/adma202300714-math-0014.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:09359648:media:adma202300714:adma202300714-math-0014" display="inline" location="graphic/adma202300714-math-0014.png">
                     <semantics>
                        <mrow>
                           <mn>22</mn>
                           <mover>
                              <mn>3</mn>
                              <mo>¯</mo>
                           </mover>
                        </mrow>
                        <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">$22\overline{3}$</annotation>
                     </semantics></math> reflections at a <i>d</i>-spacing of around 2.504, 2.443, and 2.077&nbsp;Å, respectively, which is different from single peaks of the calculated pattern (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6</a>, Supporting Information). The fact suggests slightly different lattice parameters of <i>a</i> and <i>b</i>, and an orthorhombic or monoclinic lattice instead of the abovementioned hexagonal one. Due to the presence of stacking defects as indicated by broader 1/6 2/3 1 reflection than 1/6 2/3 0 reflection (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202300714-fig-0004">4b</a>), it was difficult to perform more precise structural refinements for the ND and XRD patterns.</p>
               
               <p>Instead, the SAED pattern of Non-sub along the 001 zone axis proves clear differences in symmetry between <i>a</i>- and <i>b</i>-axes (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202300714-fig-0004">4d</a>). When the diffraction spots are indexed with the <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/bddd0166-3aa6-4c53-9e37-59ac993b5c73/adma202300714-math-0015.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:09359648:media:adma202300714:adma202300714-math-0015" display="inline" location="graphic/adma202300714-math-0015.png">
                     <semantics>
                        <mrow>
                           <mn>2</mn>
                           <msqrt>
                              <mn>3</mn>
                           </msqrt>
                           <mrow>
                              <mo>×</mo>
                              <mn>2</mn>
                           </mrow>
                           <msqrt>
                              <mn>3</mn>
                           </msqrt>
                        </mrow>
                        <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">${\rm{2}}\sqrt {\rm{3}} {\rm{ \times 2}}\sqrt {\rm{3}} $</annotation>
                     </semantics></math> superlattice model, <i>hk</i>0 (<i>h, k</i> = ±2<i>n</i>) spots such as <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/fab6d2d5-8b46-4620-bb4f-1a8868373ec9/adma202300714-math-0016.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:09359648:media:adma202300714:adma202300714-math-0016" display="inline" location="graphic/adma202300714-math-0016.png">
                     <semantics>
                        <mrow>
                           <mn>220</mn>
                        </mrow>
                        <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">$\hskip.001pt 220$</annotation>
                     </semantics></math> and <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/9a9874dd-0272-4f7b-adf1-b9eb0257f3c1/adma202300714-math-0017.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:09359648:media:adma202300714:adma202300714-math-0017" display="inline" location="graphic/adma202300714-math-0017.png">
                     <semantics>
                        <mrow>
                           <mover>
                              <mn>2</mn>
                              <mo>¯</mo>
                           </mover>
                           <mn>40</mn>
                        </mrow>
                        <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">$\overline{2}40$</annotation>
                     </semantics></math> reflections are remarkably bright and have six-fold rotational symmetry, typically observed for the P2-type (<i>γ</i>-type) Na<i><sub>x</sub></i>CoO<sub>2</sub> (<i>P</i>6<sub>3</sub>/<i>mmc</i>) phase with randomly occupied Na sites.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202300714-bib-0034" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202300714-bib-0034_R_d60345409e1856" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 34 - Phys. Rev. B: Condens. Matter Mater. Phys.">34</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Between the bright spots, characteristic additional small spots are observed. For example, the spots of 200 and 400 reflections and those of 020 and 040 ones are observed along <i>a</i>* and <i>b</i>* axis, respectively. However, <i>h</i>00 (<i>h</i> = ±(6<i>n</i> ± 1)) reflection spots such as 100 and 500 are not seen along the <i>a</i>* axis despite 0<i>k</i>0 (<i>k</i> = ±(6<i>n</i> ± 1)) spots such as 050 and 070 are visible along <i>b</i>* axis. This indicates that the symmetry elements of the <i>a</i>- and <i>b</i>-axes are different, and the lattice might not be hexagonal at room temperature. Even if the lattice is hexagonal, the <i>hk</i>0 (<i>h</i> = ±2<i>n</i>, <i>k</i> = ±(6<i>n</i> ± 1)) satellite spots such as the 050/070 and 210/230 reflections observed to the left and right around the bright <i>hk</i>0 (<i>h</i>, <i>k</i> = ±2<i>n</i>) spots cannot be indexed with a conventional Ni–Mn ordered <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/ce806845-8abe-46c9-93e7-866065371612/adma202300714-math-0018.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:09359648:media:adma202300714:adma202300714-math-0018" display="inline" location="graphic/adma202300714-math-0018.png">
                     <semantics>
                        <mrow>
                           <msqrt>
                              <mn>3</mn>
                           </msqrt>
                           <mi>a</mi>
                           <mo>×</mo>
                           <mrow></mrow>
                           <msqrt>
                              <mn>3</mn>
                           </msqrt>
                           <mi>b</mi>
                           <mspace width="0.33em"></mspace>
                        </mrow>
                        <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">$\sqrt{3}a\ensuremath{\times{}}\sqrt{3}b\ $</annotation>
                     </semantics></math> superlattice but can be indexed with a <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/19dc3f14-87ca-44eb-b467-84b9f61035df/adma202300714-math-0019.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:09359648:media:adma202300714:adma202300714-math-0019" display="inline" location="graphic/adma202300714-math-0019.png">
                     <semantics>
                        <mrow>
                           <mn>2</mn>
                           <msqrt>
                              <mn>3</mn>
                           </msqrt>
                           <mi>a</mi>
                           <mo>×</mo>
                           <mrow></mrow>

                              <mn>3</mn>
