                     </semantics></math> superlattice. The fact indicates the presence of Na<sup>+</sup>-vacancy ordering with a pseudo <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/96ea25e4-a9f3-40d3-bda3-044179147e33/adma202300714-math-0020.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:09359648:media:adma202300714:adma202300714-math-0020" display="inline" location="graphic/adma202300714-math-0020.png">
                     <semantics>
                        <mrow>
                           <mn>2</mn>
                           <msqrt>
                              <mn>3</mn>
                           </msqrt>
                           <mi>a</mi>
                           <mo>×</mo>
                           <mrow></mrow>
                           <mn>2</mn>
                           <msqrt>
                              <mn>3</mn>
                           </msqrt>
                           <mi>b</mi>
                           <mspace width="0.33em"></mspace>
                        </mrow>
                        <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">$2\sqrt{3}a\ensuremath{\times{}}2\sqrt{3}b\ $</annotation>
                     </semantics></math> superlattice in Non-sub.</p>
               
               <p>When the ND and XRD patterns of Ti-substituted samples Na<sub>2/3</sub>[Ni<sub>1/3</sub>Mn<sub>2/3−</sub><i><sub>y</sub></i>Ti<i><sub>y</sub></i>]O<sub>2</sub> (<i>y</i> = 1/6, 1/3, and 2/3) are compared with those of Non-sub in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202300714-fig-0004">4a,b</a>, the peak intensity of the 1/6 2/3 <i>l</i> (<i>l</i> = 0 and 1) reflections, which are attributed to the Na<sup>+</sup>-vacancy ordering in the superstructure <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/c62c2805-ae86-4931-9b99-4044fbf2ef7c/adma202300714-math-0021.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:09359648:media:adma202300714:adma202300714-math-0021" display="inline" location="graphic/adma202300714-math-0021.png">
                     <semantics>
                        <mrow>
                           <mn>2</mn>
                           <msqrt>
                              <mn>3</mn>
                           </msqrt>
                           <mi>a</mi>
                           <mo>×</mo>
                           <mrow></mrow>
                           <mn>2</mn>
                           <msqrt>
                              <mn>3</mn>
                           </msqrt>
                           <mi>b</mi>
                           <mo>×</mo>
                           <mrow></mrow>
                           <mi>c</mi>
                        </mrow>
                        <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">$2\sqrt{3}a\ensuremath{\times{}}2\sqrt{3}b\ensuremath{\times{}}c$</annotation>
                     </semantics></math>, decreases with increase in the Ti content. Simultaneously, the 1/3 1/3 <i>l</i> reflections also become weaker. In Na<sub>2/3</sub>[Ni<sub>1/3</sub>Ti<sub>2/3</sub>]O<sub>2</sub> (<i>y</i> = 2/3), the absence of superlattice peaks is the same result reported by Paulsen,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202300714-bib-0030" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202300714-bib-0030_R_d60345409e2020" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 30 - Chem. Mater.">30</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> suggesting the absence of Na<sup>+</sup>-vacancy or Ni-Ti in-plane ordering. This is a similar trend for cobalt substitution for nickel in Na<sub>2/3</sub>[Ni<sub>1/3</sub>Mn<sub>2/3</sub>]O<sub>2</sub>, Na<sub>2/3</sub>[Ni<sub>1/3−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>Co<i><sub>x</sub></i>Mn<sub>2/3</sub>]O<sub>2</sub>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202300714-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202300714-bib-0031_R_d60345409e2050" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 31 - Chem. Mater.">31</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Consequently, substitution of Ti<sup>IV</sup> having an intermediate ionic radius (0.605 Å at a octahedral site<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202300714-bib-0035" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202300714-bib-0035_R_d60345409e2057" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 35 - Acta Crystallogr">35</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup>) between Ni<sup>II</sup> (0.69 Å) and Mn<sup>IV</sup> (0.53 Å) simultaneously disturbs both Ni–Mn and Na<sup>+</sup>-vacancy ordering. In the slightly Ti-substituted sample of Ti-sub (<i>y</i> = 1/6), the 1/6 2/3 <i>l</i> superlattice peaks are weak but certainly observed in the ND pattern (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202300714-fig-0004">4a</a>), but the 1/6 2/3 <i>l</i> peaks as well as 1/3 1/3 <i>l</i> ones are very broad, indicating the presence of stacking faults in the structure having Ni–Mn and Na<sup>+</sup>-vacancy ordering. In the SAED pattern of Ti-sub (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202300714-fig-0004">4e</a>), six-fold rotating blurry satellite spots are observed surrounding the bright spot, which is obviously different from the satellite spots that are sharply visible only on the left and right sides of the bright spot in Non-sub. The high-resolution ND pattern of Ti-sub, indeed, shows single peaks of <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/5124536e-b998-4be8-ab6e-278d21a5d4e6/adma202300714-math-0022.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:09359648:media:adma202300714:adma202300714-math-0022" display="inline" location="graphic/adma202300714-math-0022.png">
                     <semantics>
                        <mrow>
                           <mn>220</mn>
                        </mrow>
                        <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">$\hskip.001pt 220$</annotation>
                     </semantics></math>, <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/54ce8c3a-9304-4967-8b47-f8257407d2af/adma202300714-math-0023.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:09359648:media:adma202300714:adma202300714-math-0023" display="inline" location="graphic/adma202300714-math-0023.png">
                     <semantics>
                        <mrow>
                           <mn>22</mn>
                           <mover>
                              <mn>1</mn>
                              <mo>¯</mo>
                           </mover>
                        </mrow>
                        <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">$22\overline{1}$</annotation>
                     </semantics></math>, and <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/7b9a2af9-f24d-4c38-846b-895bccedbc64/adma202300714-math-0024.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:09359648:media:adma202300714:adma202300714-math-0024" display="inline" location="graphic/adma202300714-math-0024.png">
                     <semantics>
                        <mrow>
                           <mn>22</mn>
                           <mover>
                              <mn>3</mn>
                              <mo>¯</mo>
                           </mover>
                        </mrow>
                        <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">$22\overline{3}$</annotation>
                     </semantics></math> reflections and confirms a hexagonal symmetry (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S7</a>, Supporting Information). Consequently, complete replacement of Mn<sup>IV</sup> by Ti<sup>IV</sup> is found to disrupt both Ni<sup>II</sup>-Ti<sup>IV</sup> and Na<sup>+</sup>-vacancy ordering, and slight Ti-substitution has led to a hexagonal lattice symmetry with Na<sup>+</sup>-vacancy ordering but with a high degree of stacking defects in the structure.</p>
               
               <p>Finally, Ti–Zn dual-doping effect is discussed with comparing the ND, XRD, and SAED patterns of Non-sub, Ti-sub, and Ti-Zn-sub. As seen for the Ti-sub, the peak intensity of the 1/6 2/3 <i>l</i> superlattice peaks is quite lower for Ti-Zn-sub than Non-sub, proving a suppressed Na<sup>+</sup>-vacancy ordering in Ti-Zn-sub. Differing from the Ti-sub, the 1/3 1/3 <i>l</i> superlattice peaks get rather stronger and sharper than Non-sub (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202300714-fig-0004">4a</a>), evidencing highly ordered structure including the Ni–Mn ordering and less stacking faults. These results suggest that the peak intensity of the 1/6 2/3 <i>l</i> reflection is weak in Ti-Zn-sub, which is almost the same as in the Ti-sub case, but the cause is quite different.&nbsp;Furthermore, in the SAED pattern of Ti-Zn-sub (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202300714-fig-0004">4f</a>), satellite spots surrounding the bright <i>hk</i>0 (<i>h</i>, <i>k</i> = ±2<i>n</i>) spots are quite weak, while small spots such as 200, 400, 020, and 040 reflections, corresponding to the <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/1c9d5018-7eff-4979-a947-66b866c3365d/adma202300714-math-0025.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:09359648:media:adma202300714:adma202300714-math-0025" display="inline" location="graphic/adma202300714-math-0025.png">
                     <semantics>
                        <mrow>
                           <msqrt>
                              <mn>3</mn>
                           </msqrt>
                           <mo>×</mo>
                           <msqrt>
                              <mn>3</mn>
                           </msqrt>
                        </mrow>
                        <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">$\sqrt {\rm{3}} {\rm{ \times }}\sqrt {\rm{3}} $</annotation>
                     </semantics></math> superlattice with a Ni–Mn ordering, are clearly observed. These are consistent with the results of ND and XRD. Moreover, single peaks of <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/501af266-2625-4681-bfe6-8ea6aaec712a/adma202300714-math-0026.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:09359648:media:adma202300714:adma202300714-math-0026" display="inline" location="graphic/adma202300714-math-0026.png">
                     <semantics>
                        <mrow>
                           <mn>220</mn>
                        </mrow>
                        <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">$\hskip.001pt 220$</annotation>
                     </semantics></math>, <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/e45cae1b-2f6f-4872-83d8-ebde6e712751/adma202300714-math-0027.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:09359648:media:adma202300714:adma202300714-math-0027" display="inline" location="graphic/adma202300714-math-0027.png">
                     <semantics>
                        <mrow>
                           <mn>22</mn>
                           <mover>
                              <mn>1</mn>
                              <mo>¯</mo>
                           </mover>
                        </mrow>
                        <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">$22\overline{1}$</annotation>
                     </semantics></math>, and <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/42e833d2-eee4-4599-a083-a922906021ea/adma202300714-math-0028.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:09359648:media:adma202300714:adma202300714-math-0028" display="inline" location="graphic/adma202300714-math-0028.png">
                     <semantics>
                        <mrow>
                           <mn>22</mn>
                           <mover>
                              <mn>3</mn>
                              <mo>¯</mo>
                           </mover>
                        </mrow>
                        <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">$22\overline{3}$</annotation>
                     </semantics></math> reflections are also confirmed in the ND pattern (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S7</a>, Supporting Information) and can be indexed with a hexagonal unit cell. Since the diffraction peaks/spots of 1/6 2/3 <i>l</i> reflection are quite weak but certainly observed in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202300714-fig-0004">4a,b,f</a>, a <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/da370653-f662-4f6a-a49b-a78a4abaa7b8/adma202300714-math-0029.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:09359648:media:adma202300714:adma202300714-math-0029" display="inline" location="graphic/adma202300714-math-0029.png">
                     <semantics>
                        <mrow>
                           <mn>2</mn>
                           <msqrt>
                              <mn>3</mn>
                           </msqrt>
                           <mi>a</mi>
                           <mo>×</mo>
                           <mrow></mrow>
                           <mn>2</mn>
                           <msqrt>
                              <mn>3</mn>
                           </msqrt>
                           <mi>b</mi>
                           <mo>×</mo>
                           <mrow></mrow>
                           <mi>c</mi>
                        </mrow>
                        <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">$2\sqrt{3}a\ensuremath{\times{}}2\sqrt{3}b\ensuremath{\times{}}c$</annotation>
                     </semantics></math> superstructure unit cell same as the case of Non-sub (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202300714-fig-0004">4c</a>) was used for Rietveld refinement of the synchrotron XRD and ND patterns (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S8</a>, Supporting Information). We have succeeded in fitting the experimental data of XRD and ND with satisfactory reliability factors of the refinement as shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202300714-fig-0004">4g</a> and Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a> (Supporting Information). The refined structure uniquely proves the almost Na-disordered (widely and randomly Na-distributed) and Ni–Mn ordered <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/1af63e65-1d4d-4ff6-b927-e5d6a2c91647/adma202300714-math-0030.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:09359648:media:adma202300714:adma202300714-math-0030" display="inline" location="graphic/adma202300714-math-0030.png">
                     <semantics>
                        <mrow>
                           <mn>2</mn>
                           <msqrt>
                              <mn>3</mn>
                           </msqrt>
                           <mi>a</mi>
                           <mo>×</mo>
                           <mrow></mrow>
                           <mn>2</mn>
                           <msqrt>
                              <mn>3</mn>
                           </msqrt>
                           <mi>b</mi>
                           <mo>×</mo>
                           <mrow></mrow>
                           <mi>c</mi>
                        </mrow>
                        <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">$2\sqrt{3}a\ensuremath{\times{}}2\sqrt{3}b\ensuremath{\times{}}c$</annotation>
                     </semantics></math> superlattice for Ti-Zn-sub. Although multi-element substitution is generally thought to result in a disordered arrangement of metals in a slab, dual-substitution with Ti<sup>IV</sup> and Zn<sup>II</sup> uniquely promotes Ni<sup>II</sup>–Mn<sup>IV</sup> ordering.</p>
               </section>
            
            <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adma202300714-sec-0080">
               
               <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section3" id="adma202300714-sec-0080-title">2.2.2 Water Stability and Effect of ZnO Impurity</h4>
               
               <p>Lu et al. pointed out high water-stability for in-plane Ni–Mn ordered materials,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202300714-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202300714-bib-0006_R_d60345409e2273" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - Chem. Mater.">6</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> although the water resistance mechanism applicable to all layered oxides is still debatable.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202300714-bib-0036" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202300714-bib-0036_R_d60345409e2278" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 36 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">36</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> To test water stability of Ti-Zn-sub and any effect of the ZnO impurity phase on the electrochemical performance, ZnO particles were removed from the Ti-Zn-sub powder sample through treatment with aqueous sodium hydroxide solution because ZnO is generally known to be soluble in basic NaOH aqueous solution. We also prepared a sample that was treated with deionized water to check its stability in water.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202300714-bib-0037" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202300714-bib-0037_R_d60345409e2283" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 37 - J. Electrochem. Soc.">37</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> <b>Figure</b> <a href="#adma202300714-fig-0005">5a,b</a> displays the XRD patterns of the treated Ti-Zn-sub samples. Diffraction peaks of a crystalline ZnO phase are observed for the as-prepared and water-treated samples but completely disappear for the NaOH<sub>aq.</sub>-treated sample. Energy-dispersive X-ray spectrometry (EDS) mappings for Zn also prove that ZnO particles are removed by treating with NaOH solution as shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202300714-fig-0005">5c</a> (see EDS mappings for other elements in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S9</a>, Supporting Information). Inductively coupled plasma atomic emission spectrometry (ICP-AES) results reveal slight decrease of Zn amount in the solid-solution from 0.082 to 0.056 for the Ti-Zn-sub sample after the treatment with the NaOH aqueous solution as shown in Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a> (Supporting Information). Even after treating with deionized water, the XRD peak intensity and peak position of the P2 type phase are almost the same as those of the untreated Ti-Zn-sub sample, indicating the high stability of the Ti-Zn-sub sample in water. Although the reversible capacities of both treated samples are slightly lower, their cycle stability is comparable to that of the untreated sample, confirming no apparent effects of the impurity phase of ZnO as well as the aqueous wet process on the electrochemical performance as shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202300714-fig-0005">5d</a> and Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S10</a> (Supporting Information).</p>
               
               <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
                  <figure class="figure" id="adma202300714-fig-0005"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/98b55217-e6ca-490e-80f5-c2289fce6f17/adma202300714-fig-0005-m.jpg"><picture>
                           <source srcset="/cms/asset/98b55217-e6ca-490e-80f5-c2289fce6f17/adma202300714-fig-0005-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/bcae6497-5fcc-4f24-905d-139a3a5d66c4/adma202300714-fig-0005-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/98b55217-e6ca-490e-80f5-c2289fce6f17/adma202300714-fig-0005-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                        <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 5<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma202300714-fig-0005&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.202300714" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                        </div>
                        <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) Whole and b) narrow angle ranges of XRD patterns, and c) SEM-EDS mapping of Zn element for the Ti-Zn-sub samples in the states as-synthesized, treated with distilled water, and treated with NaOH aqueous solution. d) Cycle stability of the electrodes of the untreated and treated Ti-Zn-sub samples.</div>
                     </figcaption>
                  </figure>
               </section>
               <p>From the above results and discussion, we found that Ti–Zn dual-substitution retains in-plane Ni–Mn ordering as well as water stability and suppresses Na<sup>+</sup>-vacancy ordering as well as stacking faults. One can see the remarkable effect of suppressed Na<sup>+</sup>-vacancy ordering in the sloping and smooth voltage curves compared to those of Ti-sub (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202300714-fig-0003">3</a>). The stepless curves indicate a least structural changes and phase transition during charging and discharging, which is expected to explain the longer cycle-life and higher rate performances. We further conduct operando XRD to elucidate exact phase transformation during charge–discharge processes.</p>
               </section>
            
            <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adma202300714-sec-0090">
               
               <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section3" id="adma202300714-sec-0090-title">2.2.3 Structural Evolution During Charge–Discharge</h4>
               
               <p>To understand the relationship between the voltage curves and structural evolution of the layered oxides, operando XRD was carried out for Non-sub, Ti-sub, and Ti-Zn-sub electrodes during 1st charge and discharge at a low current rate of C/50 as shown in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#adma202300714-fig-0006">6</a> and Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S11–S13</a> (Supporting Information). In the operando XRD patterns of Ti-sub (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S12</a>, Supporting Information), there is a lack of data during charging, which is due to temporary trouble of the diffractometer and not due to the electrochemical test. As expected from the almost non-stepped charge–discharge curves of Ti-Zn-sub, the operando XRD patterns (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202300714-fig-0006">6a</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S13</a>, Supporting Information) show almost monotonous and continuous peak-shift of diffraction peaks. In contrast, as several voltage steps are seen in the charge–discharge curves (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S11a</a>, Supporting Information), Non-sub electrode represents repetitive intensity-gain/-reduction of diffraction peaks in the operando XRD patterns (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S11b</a>, Supporting Information) in the voltage regions below the high-voltage plateau located at 4.20&nbsp;V on charging and at 4.06&nbsp;V on discharging (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S11a</a>, Supporting Information). These variation in diffraction intensity and peak position are not highly remarkable but the observed repetitive intensity-gain/-reduction indicates several two-phase reactions. A slight but repetitive increase/decrease in the intensity of the diffraction peaks is also observed for the Ti-sub electrode (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S12</a>, Supporting Information), as expected from the observation of satellite spots attributed to Na<sup>+</sup>-vacancy ordering in the SAED pattern (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202300714-fig-0004">4e</a>).</p>
               
               <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
                  <figure class="figure" id="adma202300714-fig-0006"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/14084f77-110c-4abc-a545-371cb9f7cc6f/adma202300714-fig-0006-m.jpg"><picture>
                           <source srcset="/cms/asset/14084f77-110c-4abc-a545-371cb9f7cc6f/adma202300714-fig-0006-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/be861384-757a-426d-8dfa-6cc6e35ded2c/adma202300714-fig-0006-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/14084f77-110c-4abc-a545-371cb9f7cc6f/adma202300714-fig-0006-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                        <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 6<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma202300714-fig-0006&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.202300714" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                        </div>
                        <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) Contour plots of operando XRD patterns and b) simultaneously collected voltage curve of the Ti-Zn-sub electrode during the initial charging process. c–e)&nbsp;Selected operando XRD patterns of Non-sub (c), Ti-sub (d), and Ti-Zn-sub (e) electrodes. f) Schematic illustrations of structural change mechanism.</div>
                     </figcaption>
                  </figure>
               </section>
               <p>More distinct differences are found in the high voltage region above 4.0&nbsp;V (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202300714-fig-0006">6c–f</a>). Non-sub electrode clearly exhibits disappearance of 002<sub>P2</sub> reflection and simultaneous appearance of 002<sub>O2</sub> reflection without a peak shift during charging in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202300714-fig-0006">6c</a>, suggesting a two-phasic reaction from P2 type to O2 type transition in the flat voltage region as reported in the literature.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202300714-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202300714-bib-0007_R_d60345409e2419" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - J. Electrochem. Soc.">7</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Ti-sub electrode also shows disappearance of 002<sub>P2</sub> on charging, but a simultaneously appeared peak is very broad and weak in intensity (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202300714-fig-0006">6d</a>). Furthermore, despite the flat voltage region at ≈4.25&nbsp;V on the 1st charging (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S12a</a>, Supporting Information), the newly appearing peak around 2<i>θ</i> = 17° gradually shifts toward higher diffraction angle. The observed broad and weak peak indicates presence of multiple phases and/or lower periodicity of the slabs stacking including a high degree of stacking faults. On the contrary, Ti-Zn-sub electrode retains sharp diffraction peaks above 4.0&nbsp;V (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202300714-fig-0006">6e</a>). Unlike Non-sub and Ti-sub, a new peak is observed around 2<i>θ</i> = 16.2° between the two peaks at 2<i>θ</i> = 15.7° and 17° (see the enlarged patterns in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S13c</a>, Supporting Information). The fact indicates possible formation of an intermediate intergrowth between P2 and O2 types such as OP4 type<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202300714-bib-0038" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202300714-bib-0038_R_d60345409e2446" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 38 - Nat. Mater.">38</a>-<a href="#adma202300714-bib-0040" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202300714-bib-0040_R_d60345409e2446" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 40 - Energy Environ. Sci.">40</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> and a more coherent slab-stacking than Ti-sub.</p>
               
               <p>When synchrotron XRD patterns of the fully charged electrodes are compared among the three samples as shown in Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S14a</a> (Supporting Information), the main phase of all the three samples is found to be O2 type phase. However, their lattice parameters are different, which is confirmed as different peak positions of 002 and 110 reflections in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S14b</a> (Supporting Information). It should be noted that the broadening of the 10<i>l</i> reflections is due to the stacking faults of the TM slabs, because it is equally possible to form two different O2-type structures from a P2-type one by two different gliding vectors of the TM slabs.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202300714-bib-0039" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202300714-bib-0039_R_d60345409e2462" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 39 - J. Electrochem. Soc.">39</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Even in the synchrotron XRD pattern (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S14</a>, Supporting Information), 002 peak of the Ti-sub electrode is much broader than the other two samples, which is consistent with the operando XRD patterns in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202300714-fig-0006">6d</a>. A minor phase having smaller <i>a</i> and <i>c</i> lattice parameters should co-exist in the charged Ti-sub electrode. When diffraction peaks are indexed with a conventional small hexagonal lattice, the lattice parameters of the samples charged to 4.5&nbsp;V are calculated as <i>a</i> = 2.845 Å and <i>c</i> = 8.877 Å for Non-sub, <i>a</i> = 2.83 Å and <i>c</i> = 10.1 Å for Ti-sub, and <i>a</i> = 2.862 Å and <i>c</i> = 10.768 Å for Ti-Zn-sub. The interslab distance of the hexagonal O2 type phage for Non-sub, Ti-sub, and Ti-Zn-sub is 4.438, 5.05, and 5.384 Å, respectively. The Ti–Zn dual substitution successfully suppresses the interslab shrinkage.</p>
               
               <p>From the above discussion, we schematically summarize structural change mechanism in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202300714-fig-0006">6f</a>. By Na extraction in Non-sub, O2 type domains are grown with disappearance of P2 type domains, resulting in the P2–O2 transition with distinct phase separation. In contrast, Ti-sub has a wider distribution of the TM slabs with different Na concentrations in the interslab space during charging above 4.0&nbsp;V. The stacking defects induced by Ti substitution, which is confirmed with the ND and SAED patterns, would lead to random Na extraction, resulting in disturbing the phase separation between P2 and O2 types and/or destabilization of intermediate P–O phases. In the case of Ti–Zn dual-substitution, X-ray absorption near-edge structure (XANES) data represent reversible energy shift of only Ni K-edge during charging and discharging and no significant energy shift for Mn, Ti, and Zn K-edge (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#adma202300714-fig-0007">7</a>). The fact suggests that nickel redox mainly participates in the redox reaction and Zn<sup>II</sup> is not oxidized by charging and would attract sodium ions in the structure as an electrochemically inactive metal (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202300714-fig-0007">7d</a>). Furthermore, the Zn K-edge spectra, which show a reversible return to the initial state, indicate that Zn is not migrated, such as irreversible extraction on charging. The Ni K-edge energy of Ti-Zn-sub at 4.5&nbsp;V is very close to that of the reference material, LaNi<sup>III</sup>O<sub>3</sub>. As recent papers reported for Non-sub with O K-edge XAS and resonant inelastic X-ray scattering (RIXS) spectra,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202300714-bib-0041" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202300714-bib-0041_R_d60345409e2512" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 41 - Nano Energy">41</a>, <a href="#adma202300714-bib-0042" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202300714-bib-0042_R_d60345409e2512" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 42 - Nano Energy">42</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> only the nickel redox cannot explain charging and discharge capacities. Lattice oxygen should take part in the charge compensation mechanism of Ti-Zn-sub during charging and discharging as an anion redox without oxygen gas generation. Thus, oxidation of nickel and oxide ions during charging results in reducing the averaged Na concentration in the interslab space, changing the structures from a widely spaced P2-type structure to a narrowly spaced O2-type one. Nevertheless, the O type layer in the O2 type phase of Ti-Zn-sub has wider interslab space than the other two samples. Formation of the wider interslab space of the O type layer would facilitate faster P2–O2 transition in Ti-Zn-sub with keeping the high crystallinity along the stacking direction. Furthermore, the presence of the P2–O2 intermediate phase observed in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202300714-fig-0006">6e</a> would compensate for the small but present lattice mismatch between the P2 and O2 phases.</p>
               
               <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
                  <figure class="figure" id="adma202300714-fig-0007"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/0ef85dc0-f935-4b87-90c5-a84ef1a5f936/adma202300714-fig-0007-m.jpg"><picture>
                           <source srcset="/cms/asset/0ef85dc0-f935-4b87-90c5-a84ef1a5f936/adma202300714-fig-0007-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/2784636f-89d2-4692-b18c-9ef2394a6759/adma202300714-fig-0007-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/0ef85dc0-f935-4b87-90c5-a84ef1a5f936/adma202300714-fig-0007-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                        <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 7<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma202300714-fig-0007&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.202300714" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                        </div>
                        <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a–d) Ex situ XANES spectra of the Ti-Zn-sub electrodes at Ni K-edge (a), Mn K-edge (b), Ti K-edge (c), and Zn K-edge (d). The Ni K-edge spectrum of LaNiO<sub>3</sub> as a standard reference is shown in (a).</div>
                     </figcaption>
                  </figure>
               </section>
               <p>Large repetitive changes in interslab distance (or volume) during charging and discharging are known to cause cracks in the particles, resulting in capacity degradation during cycling, as reported for Non-sub with a 23% shrinkage in volume.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202300714-bib-0043" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202300714-bib-0043_R_d60345409e2546" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 43 - Nano Energy">43</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Based on the synchrotron XRD results (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S14</a>, Supporting Information), shrinkage ratio in volume between the pristine and fully charged states is 13% for Ti-sub and 7% for Ti-Zn-sub. The suppressed volume shrinkage can explain the better cycle stability and rate capability for Ti-sub and Ti-Zn-sub samples.</p>
               </section>
            
            <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adma202300714-sec-0100">
               
               <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section3" id="adma202300714-sec-0100-title">2.2.4 Particle Surface Morphology after Cycling</h4>
               
               <p>To confirm the metal-substitution effect on the particle surface after cycling, ex situ TEM observation was performed for the Non-sub, Ti-sub, and Ti-Zn-sub electrodes after 10 cycles (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#adma202300714-fig-0008">8</a>). Although large cracks breaking particles are not observed after 10 cycles, corrosive pits with 15–20&nbsp;nm in depth are seen at the outermost surface of Non-sub particles as previously reported.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202300714-bib-0044" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202300714-bib-0044_R_d60345409e2568" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 44 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">44</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Ti-sub also shows corrosive pits at the outermost surface, but the pits are fewer and shallower (≈10&nbsp;nm) than those of Non-sub. Surprisingly, no corrosive pits are observed for Ti-Zn-sub particles, indicating the stable particle surface during cycling. Similar to the case of P2-Na<sub>2/3</sub>[Al<sub>1/18</sub>Ni<sub>11/36</sub>Mn<sub>23/36</sub>]O<sub>2</sub>,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202300714-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202300714-bib-0015_R_d60345409e2584" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - Adv. Energy Mater.">15</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> the stable surface would result in the excellent performance of Ti-Zn-sub.</p>
               
               <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
                  <figure class="figure" id="adma202300714-fig-0008"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/986d3ff8-055b-4d8f-875e-244c2dbbee66/adma202300714-fig-0008-m.jpg"><picture>
                           <source srcset="/cms/asset/986d3ff8-055b-4d8f-875e-244c2dbbee66/adma202300714-fig-0008-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/c66bdead-6bb8-4cef-a439-0066fc71a812/adma202300714-fig-0008-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/986d3ff8-055b-4d8f-875e-244c2dbbee66/adma202300714-fig-0008-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                        <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 8<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma202300714-fig-0008&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.202300714" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                        </div>
                        <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">TEM images of the Non-sub, Ti-sub, and Ti-Zn-sub electrode samples in the pristine (left) and 10 cycled (right) states.</div>
                     </figcaption>
                  </figure>
               </section>
               <p>The Ti–Zn dual-substituted layered oxide retains the original wide interslab space during charging, contributing to suppression of the structural stress between the P2 and O2 phases and stabilization of the crystal structures in both bulk and surface. In the present study, high concentration of Ti or Zn was not found on the outermost surface, but doped Ti possibly protect the particle surface against HF attack during electrolyte decomposition at high voltage as reported for NaMn<sub>0.8</sub>Ti<sub>0.1</sub>Ni<sub>0.1</sub>O<sub>2</sub>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202300714-bib-0045" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202300714-bib-0045_R_d60345409e2619" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 45 - Nat. Commun.">45</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In the present Ti-Zn-sub sample, the excess Zn is not present as a thin ZnO coating layer with a few nanometers in thickness,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202300714-bib-0046" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202300714-bib-0046_R_d60345409e2625" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 46 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">46</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> and is contained as ZnO particles of several hundred nanometers. Further optimizing of synthesis conditions such as Zn content, starting materials, precursors, and calcination temperature and time would reduce electrochemically inactive and insulating ZnO particles and the optimal P2-Na<i><sub>x</sub></i>[Ni, Mn, Ti, Zn]O<sub>2</sub> is expected to demonstrate further improved electrochemical properties. Furthermore, we hope that the findings of this study will lead to the understanding that conventional metal substitution, which is a disorderly mixing of metals in a slab, is not always the best method, and that the further development of metal substitution methods yield promising positive electrode materials for Na-ion batteries.</p>
               </section>
            </section>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adma202300714-sec-0110">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adma202300714-sec-0110-title">3 Conclusion</h2>
         
         <p>In Na<sub>2/3</sub>[Ni<sub>1/3−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>Mn<sub>2/3−</sub><i><sub>y</sub></i>Me<i><sub>x</sub></i>Ti<i><sub>y</sub></i>]O<sub>2</sub> (Me = Mg, Cu, Zn), the effects of substitution by titanium and divalent metals of Mg, Cu, and Zn on the crystal structure and electrochemical properties have been studied. The Ti–Zn and Ti-Cu dual-substituted samples demonstrate superior cycle stability and rate capability in non-aqueous Na cells in the voltage range of 2.5–4.5&nbsp;V. The novel moisture-stable positive electrode material of Na<sub>2/3</sub>Ni<sub>1/4</sub>Mn<sub>1/2</sub>Ti<sub>1/6</sub>Zn<sub>1/12</sub>O<sub>2</sub> exhibits non-stepwise charge–discharge voltage curves and delivers a reversible capacity of 114&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> with an average discharge voltage of 3.60&nbsp;V versus Na. Comparing the three samples of non-substituted, Ti-substituted, and Ti–Zn dual-substituted samples, synchrotron X-ray, neutron, and electron diffraction measurements reveal modified Ni–Mn and Na<sup>+</sup>-vacancy orderings by the substitution. Dual-substitution by Ti<sup>IV</sup> and Zn<sup>II</sup> maintains the Ni<sup>II</sup>–Mn<sup>IV</sup> ordering but disrupts the Na<sup>+</sup>-vacancy ordering, leading to the smooth voltage curves with less structural changes. The amount of solid-solution Zn is very small (≈0.06 per formula unit), but dual-substituting with Ti and Zn uniquely promotes Ni<sup>II</sup>–Mn<sup>IV</sup> ordering and improves electrochemical sodium (de)intercalation properties.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adma202300714-sec-0120">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adma202300714-sec-0120-title">4 Experimental Section</h2>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adma202300714-sec-0130">
            
            <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adma202300714-sec-0140">
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adma202300714-sec-0150">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="adma202300714-sec-0150-title"> Synthesis Procedures</h4>
                  
                  <p>Na<sub>2/3</sub>[Ni<sub>1/3−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>Mn<sub>2/3−</sub><i><sub>y</sub></i>Me<i><sub>x</sub></i>Ti<i><sub>y</sub></i>]O<sub>2</sub> (Me = Mg, Cu, and Zn, <i>x</i> = 1/12 for the Mg and Cu cases and <i>x</i> = 0, 1/24, 1/20, and 1/12 for the Zn case, <i>y</i> = 1/6) and Na<sub>2/3</sub>[Ni<sub>1/3</sub>Mn<sub>2/3−</sub><i><sub>y</sub></i>Ti<i><sub>y</sub></i>]O<sub>2</sub> (<i>y</i> = 0, 1/6, 1/3, and 2/3) were prepared by a solid-state reaction. Reagent grade Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub> (Nacalai Tesque, Inc., 99.8%), and NiO (FUJIFILM Wako Pure Chemical Corporation), TiO<sub>2</sub> (anatase, Sigma-Aldrich Co. LLC), MgO (Kishida Chem. Co., Ltd.), Cu<sub>2</sub>O (Kishida Chem. Co., Ltd.), ZnO (Kanto Chemical Co., Ltd.) and Mn<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> prepared by calcining MnCO<sub>3</sub> (Kishida Chem. Co., Ltd., 44% as Mn content) at 700&nbsp;°C were used as starting materials. 5 mol% excess amounts of Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub> were added in starting materials in the consideration for the high volatility of sodium oxides during the heat treatment. The starting materials were ball-milled with acetone at 600&nbsp;rpm for 12&nbsp;h by using a planetary ball mill (PULVERISETTE 7 classic line, Fritsch Japan Co., Ltd). The mixture was dried and pelletized, then heated to 900&nbsp;°C in air at a temperature increase rate of 5&nbsp;°C&nbsp;min<sup>−1</sup>, and the temperature was held for 12&nbsp;h. After the calcination, the samples were taken out from the furnace without cooling process, and then immediately transferred into an argon-filled glove box. The samples were cooled to room temperature in the glove box and were kept inside to avoid the exposure to moisture in air.</p>
                  </section>
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adma202300714-sec-0160">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="adma202300714-sec-0160-title"> Structural and Morphological Characterization</h4>
                  
                  <p>Structures of the Na<sub>2/3</sub>[Ni<sub>1/3−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>Mn<sub>2/3−</sub><i><sub>y</sub></i>Me<i><sub>x</sub></i>Ti<i><sub>y</sub></i>]O<sub>2</sub> samples were identified by using an X-ray diffractometer (SmartLab, Rigaku Corporation) equipped with a high-speed position sensitive detector (D/teX Ultra, Rigaku Corporation) and Ni-filtered Cu K<i>α</i> radiation as an X-ray source. The samples were fixed in a homemade air-tight sample holder during XRD measurements to avoid air exposure. Detailed structures of the samples were determined with a synchrotron X-ray diffractometer at BL02B2<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202300714-bib-0047" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202300714-bib-0047_R_d60345409e2808" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 47 - Rev. Sci. Instrum.">47</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> of SPring-8 in Japan, equipped with a large Debye-Scherrer camera. For ex situ measurements, samples were electrochemically prepared in the coin type cells and sodium content was calculated from the charge passed. The composite electrodes, taken out from the coin cells, were rinsed with propylene carbonate (PC, Kishida Chemical Co., Ltd.) and then with diethyl carbonate (DEC, Kishida Chemical Co., Ltd.) to remove an electrolyte salt and then dried at room temperature in an Ar-filled glovebox. The oxide composites were separated from the aluminum current collectors, crushed into powders. As-prepared and electrochemically tested samples were put into glass capillaries with 0.3 and 0.5&nbsp;mm in diameter, respectively. The glass capillaries were sealed with a resin in the glovebox or by heating and melting the end of glass capillaries to eliminate sample exposure to air. To minimize the effect of X-ray absorption by the samples, the wavelength of incident X-ray beam was set to 0.5&nbsp;Å using a silicon monochromator, which was calibrated with NIST standard reference material CeO<sub>2</sub> as an external standard reference. Structural parameters were refined by Rietveld method with the program RIETAN-FP<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202300714-bib-0048" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202300714-bib-0048_R_d60345409e2816" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 48 - Solid State Phenom.">48</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> and the program Z-Rietveld (version 1.1.3 for Windows).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202300714-bib-0049" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202300714-bib-0049_R_d60345409e2821" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 49 - Nucl. Instrum. Methods Phys. Res., Sect. A">49</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Schematic illustrations of the crystal structures of the sample were drawn using the program VESTA.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202300714-bib-0050" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202300714-bib-0050_R_d60345409e2826" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 50 - J. Appl. Crystallogr.">50</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Structural changes during an initial charging and discharging processes were examined for P2 type Na<sub>2/3</sub>[Ni<sub>1/3</sub>Mn<sub>2/3</sub>]O<sub>2</sub>, Na<sub>2/3</sub>[Ni<sub>1/3</sub>Mn<sub>1/3</sub>Ti<sub>1/6</sub>]O<sub>2</sub>, and Na<sub>2/3</sub>[Ni<sub>1/4</sub>Mn<sub>1/2</sub>Ti<sub>1/6</sub>Zn<sub>1/12</sub>]O<sub>2</sub> by an operando XRD technique using a battery-cell attachment (Rigaku Corporation)<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202300714-bib-0051" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202300714-bib-0051_R_d60345409e2864" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 51 - Rigaku J.">51</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> and the data were collected by an X-ray diffractometer (MultiFlex, Rigaku Corporation) equipped with a high-speed position sensitive detector (D/teX Ultra, Rigaku Corporation) and non-monochromatized but Ni-filtered Cu K<i>α</i> radiation as an X-ray source.</p>
                  
                  <p>Particle morphology of the samples was observed by using a scanning electron microscope (SEM, JCM-6000, JEOL Ltd.) equipped with an energy-dispersive X-ray spectrometer. The outermost surface structure and morphology of the samples were observed with the JEM-2100F transmission electron microscope (TEM, JEOL Ltd.) at an accelerating voltage of 200&nbsp;kV. Selected area electron diffraction (SAED) patterns of the samples were also collected with the microscope. The composition of the non-washed and washed Na<sub>2/3</sub>[Ni<sub>1/4</sub>Mn<sub>1/2</sub>Ti<sub>1/6</sub>Zn<sub>1/12</sub>]O<sub>2</sub> samples was confirmed by inductively coupled plasma atomic emission spectrometry (ICP-AES) using SPS3500 (Hitachi High-Tech Corporation).</p>
                  </section>
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adma202300714-sec-0170">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="adma202300714-sec-0170-title"> Electrochemical Characterization</h4>
                  
                  <p>Positive electrodes were prepared by following procedures. A slurry with a mixture of 80&nbsp;wt% Na<sub>2/3</sub>[Ni<sub>1/3−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>Mn<sub>2/3−</sub><i><sub>y</sub></i>Me<i><sub>x</sub></i>Ti<i><sub>y</sub></i>]O<sub>2</sub>, 10&nbsp;wt% acetylene black (AB, Strem Chemicals, Inc.) and 10&nbsp;wt% poly(vinylidene fluoride) (PVdF, Polysciences, Inc.) with <i>N</i>-methylpyrrolidone (Kanto Chemical Co., Ltd.) as a solvent was prepared, pasted on an aluminum foil and then dried at 80&nbsp;°C under vacuum. Single-side-coated electrodes were punched into 10 or 15&nbsp;mm disks in diameter for R2032 type coin cells with an Al-clad type of stainless-steel cap for the positive electrode side (Hohsen Corp.). The active material loading was ≈2.5&nbsp;mg&nbsp;cm<sup>−2</sup>. The separator used was glass fiber filter (GB-100R, ADVANTEC, Co.), the electrolyte solution was 1.0&nbsp;mol&nbsp;dm<sup>−3</sup> NaPF<sub>6</sub> dissolved in PC (Kishida Chemical Co., Ltd.), and the negative electrode was sodium metal (purity &gt; 99%, Kanto Chemical Co., Ltd.). The electrode preparation and fabrication of the coin cells were carried out in an Ar-filled glove box. Galvanostatic charge–discharge tests were conducted with a charge/discharge measurement system (TOSCAT-3100, TOYO System Co., Ltd.) in the voltage range of 2.5–4.5&nbsp;V at C/20 (≈13.0&nbsp;mA&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>) at 25&nbsp;°C.</p>
                  </section>
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adma202300714-sec-0180">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="adma202300714-sec-0180-title"> Hard X-ray Absorption Spectroscopy</h4>
                  
                  <p>Hard X-ray absorption spectroscopy (XAS) was performed at beam line BL-12C in the Photon Factory, High Energy Accelerator Research Organization (KEK), Japan. The spectra were collected with a silicon monochromator in a transmission mode. The intensity of incident and transmitted X-ray was measured using ionization chambers at room temperature. The energy was calibrated by setting the first inflection point in the spectrum of a Cu foil to 8979&nbsp;eV. Na-extracted electrode samples were electrochemically prepared in the coin cells. The positive electrodes were taken out from the coin cells, rinsed with PC and DEC and then dried at room temperature in an Ar-filled glovebox. The several electrodes with Al-foil current collectors were stacked and sealed in an oxygen- and moisture-barrier polymer film in the Ar-filled glovebox. The Ni K-edge spectrum of LaNiO<sub>3</sub> (99.9%, Kojundo Chemical Laboratory Co., Ltd.) mixed with BN (99%, Kanto Chemical Co., Ltd.) powder was measured as a standard reference. Absorption spectroscopy data were normalized and treated using the ATHENA program on the Demeter software package.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202300714-bib-0052" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202300714-bib-0052_R_d60345409e2937" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 52 - J. Synchrotron Radiat.">52</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Fourier transformation of the <i>χ</i>(k)<i>k</i><sup>3</sup>–<i>k</i> plots, where <i>χ</i> and <i>k</i> are oscillatory components of the normalized absorption and angular wavenumber, respectively, was performed using the ATHENA program.</p>
                  </section>
               </section>
            </section>
         </section>
      <div class="article-section__content">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adma202300714-sec-0190-title"> Acknowledgements</h2>
         
         <p>This study was in part supported by the Japan Science and Technology Agency (JST) through the Adaptable and Seamless Technology Transfer Program through Target driven R&amp;D (A-STEP), Project No. AS2614056L through the CONCERT-Japan program of the Strategic International Research Cooperative Program (SICORP), Grant No. JPMJSC17C1, through the program of Core Research for Evolutional Science and Technology (CREST), Grant No. JPMJCR21O6, and by the Element Strategy Initiative of the Ministry of Education Culture, Sports, Science and Technology, Japan (MEXT), Grant Number JPMXP0112101003. The synchrotron X-ray diffraction experiments were performed at the BL02B2 of SPring-8 with the approval of JASRI (Proposal No. 2017B1312). The neutron-scattering experiments were approved by the Neutron Science Proposal Review Committee of J-PARC/MLF (Proposal No. 2014PM0005). The synchrotron X-ray absorption work was done under the approval of the Photon Factory Program Advisory Committee (Proposal No. 2014G055 and 2018G673). This research was supported in part financially by BASF SE. The authors thank Ms. Sayuri Fukuyama, Dr. Zhen-Ji Han, and Dr. Hitoshi Fukumitsu (BASF Japan Ltd.) for their advice and fruitful discussion. The authors are grateful to Dr. Toshinari Ichihashi and Prof. Yasushi Idemoto in the Tokyo University of Science for TEM measurements.</p>
         
         <ol></ol>
      </div>
